Definition and Types of Makruh (Disliked Acts)
Defines makruh linguistically and technically, distinguishing tahrimi and tanzihi

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of makruh, distinguish between makruh tahrimi and tanzihi, and explain the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf regarding their basis.

Core Idea
Makruh is two types: tahrimi (prohibited by speculative evidence) and tanzihi (merely disliked without prohibition).

This lesson unpacks the Hanafi classification of disliked acts (makruhat). The passage defines makruh linguistically and technically, then dives into the scholarly difference between Imam Muhammad and the two shaykhs on what makes an act makruh tahrimi versus haram. Understanding this gradation is key to knowing the legal weight of an action in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic definition
The text states that makruh is the opposite of the beloved (al-makrūh ḍidd al-maḥbūb). This is the lexical meaning.
2
Identify the technical definition
The text says: 'what has a prohibition that is speculative, its dislike is prohibitive unless there is a diverting factor.' This is the shar‘ī meaning according to Imam Muhammad.
The phrase 'unless there is a diverting factor' (illā liṣārif) indicates that if a stronger evidence diverts the ruling, the act may not be tahrimi.
3
Trace the two views within the Hanafi school
Contrast Imam Muhammad’s view (any speculative prohibition makes it makruh tahrimi) with the view of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (a prohibition makes it haram; without prohibition, it is either tanzihi or tahrimi based on proximity to halal/haram).
The disagreement centers on whether a zannī dalil can make an act haram or only tahrimi.
4
Determine the consequence of makruh tahrimi
The doer of makruh tahrimi deserves a harm less than the fire, specifically deprivation of specific intercession (shafā‘ah makhṣūṣah), not absolute intercession.
Absolute intercession is for major sinners, so the makruh tahrimi offender is not deprived of that.
5
Distinguish from major sins
The text clarifies that a major sinner is not deprived of absolute intercession; thus, the consequence for makruh tahrimi is lesser. This helps calibrate the gravity of the disliked act.
This distinction prevents conflating makruh tahrimi with haram.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في المكروهات
المكروه ضد المحبوب وما كان النهي فيه ظنياً كراهته تحريمية إلا لصارف، وإن لم
قوله: (المكروه ضد المحبوب) هذا معناه لغة . قوله: (وما كان النهي فيه ظنياً) هذا معناه شرعاً أفاد السيد في التلويح أن ما كان تركه أولى فمع المنع عن الفعل بدليل قطعي حرام، وبدليل ظني مكروه كراهة التحريم، وبدون المنع عن الفعل مكروه كراهة التنزيه وهذا على رأي الإمام محمد رضي الله تعالى عنه وعلى رأي الشيخين ما يكون تركه أولى من فعله فهو مع المنع عن الفعل حرام وبدونه مكروه كراهة التنزيه إن كان إلى الحل أقرب بمعنى أنه لا يعاقب فاعله لكن يثاب تاركه أدنى ثواب وكراهة التحريم إن كان إلى الحرام أقرب بمعنى أن فاعله يستحق. محذوراً دون العقوبة بالنار كحرمان الشفاعة .اهـ. المراد منه ، والمراد بالشفاعة شفاعة مخصوصة كرفع الدرجات لا مطلق الشفاعة لأنه لا يحرمها مرتكب الكبيرة على ما صرح به قوله : شفاعتي لأهل الكبائر من أمتي فكيف مرتكب المكروه أفاده عماد الدين محشي التلويح، وذكر الخيالي في حاشية شرح العقائد ما نصه لا يقال مرتكب المكروه، ويستحق حرمان الشفاعة كما نص عليه في التلويح فيحرم أهل الكبائر بطريق الأولى لأنا نقول لا نسلم الملازمة لأن جزاء
Chapter on Disliked Acts. The disliked is the opposite of the beloved, and what has a prohibition that is speculative, its dislike is prohibitive unless there is a diverting factor, and if not... His saying: (the disliked is the opposite of the beloved) this is its linguistic meaning. His saying: (and what has a prohibition that is speculative) this is its technical meaning. The author of al-Talwih said: that which is better to leave: if accompanied by a prohibition from a definite evidence, it is haram; with speculative evidence, it is makruh tahrimi; without any prohibition, it is makruh tanzihi. This is according to Imam Muhammad. According to the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), that which is better to leave: if accompanied by a prohibition, it is haram; without it, it is makruh tanzihi if it is closer to the permissible (meaning the doer is not punished but the leaver gets minimal reward), and makruh tahrimi if it is closer to the forbidden (meaning the doer deserves a harm less than the punishment of fire, like deprivation of intercession). The intended is specific intercession for raising ranks, not absolute intercession, because the committer of a major sin is not deprived of absolute intercession as explicitly stated in the hadith: 'My intercession is for the people of major sins from my nation.' So how about the committer of a disliked act? This is mentioned by Imad al-Din, the commentator of al-Talwih. And al-Khayali in his gloss on Sharh al-Aqa'id said: one cannot say the committer of a disliked act deserves deprivation of intercession as stated in al-Talwih, because that would mean the committers of major sins deserve it a fortiori. We respond: we do not accept the entailment because the punishment...
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage from Hashiyat al-Tahtawi opens the chapter on disliked acts (makruhat) in prayer. The matn defines makruh linguistically as the opposite of beloved, and technically as that which is prohibited by a speculative (zanni) evidence, making it makruh tahrimi. The commentary then distinguishes between the views of Imam Muhammad and the two shaykhs: according to Imam Muhammad, if there is a prohibition (even with zanni dalil), the act is makruh tahrimi; if no prohibition, it is tanzihi. According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, if there is a prohibition, it is haram; if no prohibition, then it is tanzihi if closer to halal, and tahrimi if closer to haram. The text further discusses the punishment for makruh tahrimi, which includes deprivation of specific intercession (not absolute intercession, which is for major sinners). The discussion clarifies the gradations of disliked acts in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
المكروه (al-makrūh): That which is better to leave; linguistically opposite of beloved; technically: if prohibition is zanni, it is makruh tahrimi; if no prohibition, it is tanzihi.
كراهة تحريمية (karāhat taḥrīmiyya): Dislike that is proximate to prohibition; the doer deserves a harm but not the fire; e.g., deprivation of specific intercession.
كراهة تنزيهية (karāhat tanzīhiyya): Dislike that is not prohibitive; the doer is not punished but the leaver gets reward.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Muhammad's view presented in the text, what makes an act makruh tahrimi?
A) The act is closer to haram than to halal.
B) The act has a prohibition based on speculative evidence.
C) There is no prohibition, but it is better to leave.
D) The act is the opposite of the beloved.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school uniquely divides makruh into two grades: tahrimi (with zanni prohibition) and tanzihi (no prohibition).

Why Woman's Salah Breaks from Intimate Contact but Not Man's
Rational explanation of differential invalidation of salah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on invalidation of salah through intimate contact, distinguish between the cases of man and woman, and identify the legal analogy (qiyas) and the role of desire (shahwah).

Core Idea
A woman's salah breaks from a man's kiss or touch even without her desire, but a man's salah does not break from her kiss without his desire because her desire is inherently stronger and his acts are in the meaning of intercourse.

This lesson examines a nuanced ruling in Hanafi fiqh concerning the invalidation of salah through intimate contact. While both men and women can have their salah invalidated by certain acts, the ruling differs based on who initiates and the presence of desire. The passage from Hashiya al-Tahtawi clarifies the reasoning behind this distinction through a rational analogy (qiyas) to the act of intercourse.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
State the two parts: when a man touches/kisses a woman praying, her salah breaks; when a woman touches/kisses a man praying without him desiring, his salah does not break.
This is the basic ruling from Zahir al-Riwayah.
2
Locate the apparent contradiction
The text raises the question: why is there a difference when the act is similar? Identify the question posed in the passage.
The question highlights the asymmetry.
3
Analyze the rational proof (dalil aqli)
The author provides two reasons: (a) women have stronger desire (shahwah), so a man's kiss necessarily incites her desire; (b) a man's kissing/touching with desire is in the meaning of intercourse (fi ma'na al-jima') because intercourse is his act, and his preliminaries (dawa'i) are like the act itself. For a woman, intercourse is not her act, so her dawa'i are not in the meaning of intercourse unless the man desires.
This is a qiyas based on the 'illa of being in the meaning of intercourse.
4
Apply the ruling to similar scenarios
Consider other intimate acts: if a man kisses a woman without his desire but with hers? According to the ruling, if the man does not desire, his salah is not invalidated. For the woman, her desire is automatically incited by the man's act.
This shows the asymmetry based on gender and desire.

ولو كانت تصلى فأولج بين فخذيها، وإن لم ينزل أو قبلها، ولو بدون شهوة أو لمسها بشهوة فسدت صلاتها، وإن قبلته ولم تشتهها لم تفسد صلاته . فإن قلت : ما الفرق بين تقبيلها إياه، أو لمسها، وهو يصلي بغير شهوة منه، وبين تقبيله إياها، أو لمسه وهي تصلي بغير شهوة أيضاً حيث تفسد صلاتها لا صلاته. قلت: الفرق أن الشهوة فيهن أبلغ فتقبيله مستلزم لاشتهائها، وأيضاً تقبيله مطلقاً، ومسه بشهوة في معنى الجماع يعني والجماع فعل الزوج ففعله الدواعي كفعله حقيقة الجماع ولو جامعها، ولو بين . تفسد صلاتها، فكذا هذا بخلاف المرأة فإن الجماع ليس فعلها فلا يكون إتيانها بالدواعي في معنى الجماع ما لم يشته الزوج، أفاده الحلبي والله سبحانه وتعالى أعلم وأستغفر الله العظيم.
And if she was praying and he inserted between her thighs, even if he did not ejaculate, or he kissed her, even without desire, or he touched her with desire, her salah is invalidated. And if she kissed him and did not desire him, his salah is not invalidated. If you say: What is the difference between her kissing him or touching him while he is praying without desire from him, and between him kissing her or touching her while she is praying without desire as well, where her salah is invalidated but not his? I say: The difference is that desire in women is more intense, so his kissing necessarily entails her desire. Also, his kissing absolutely, and his touching with desire, are in the meaning of intercourse, meaning intercourse is the act of the husband, so his performing the motives (dawa'i) is like his performing the reality of intercourse. Even if he had intercourse with her, even if between..., her salah is invalidated. So too this. Contrary to the woman, for intercourse is not her act, so her bringing the motives is not in the meaning of intercourse unless the husband desires. This is from al-Halabi, and Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage clarifies a specific ruling regarding the invalidation of salah through intimate contact. The text first states the ruling: if a man, while praying, inserts his penis between a woman's thighs (intercourse simulation), even without orgasm, or kisses her without desire, or touches her with desire, the woman's salah is invalidated. However, if a woman kisses a man without her desiring it, his salah is not invalidated. The commentator then raises a question: why is there a difference? The answer: women have a stronger innate desire, so any kiss from the man automatically incites her desire. Moreover, the man's kissing or touching with desire is analogous to intercourse because intercourse is the man's action—his performance of preliminary acts (dawa'i) is like the act itself. For the woman, intercourse is not her act, so her engaging in such acts does not equate to intercourse unless the man is also desiring. This distinction is based on the nature of sexual desire and the legal analogy (qiyas) to intercourse. The ruling is from al-Halabi. This shows the Hanafi approach to defining legal causation ('illa) based on both physical acts and psychological states.

Key Terms
الشهوة (al-shahwah): Sexual desire or inclination, which triggers the legal analogy to intercourse.
في معنى الجماع (fi ma'na al-jima'): An act that is legally analogous to intercourse, carrying the same ruling for invalidation of salah.
دواعي (dawa'i): Preliminary acts or motives leading to intercourse, considered as part of the act itself for legal purposes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does a woman's salah become invalid if she is kissed by a man without desire from her, but a man's salah does not become invalid if he is kissed by a woman without desire from him?
A) Because the man's desire is always present in such acts.
B) Because women have stronger innate desire, so any kiss from a man necessarily incites her desire, making it analogous to intercourse.
C) Because the woman is more responsible for her actions in salah.
D) Because the hadith says so without further reason.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a woman's salah is invalidated by a man's kiss or touch due to the legal analogy to intercourse, which applies asymmetrically based on gender and the nature of desire.

Salah — Prayer
beginner
6 min
Definition and Types of Karahah in Salah
Defines makruh linguistically and legally, distinguishing tahrimi and tanzihi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between makruh tahrimi and makruh tanzihi in salah and state the consequence for repetition of salah.

Core Idea
Makruh tahrimi requires wajib repetition of salah; makruh tanzihi only recommends it.

In this passage, the author defines the disliked acts (makruhat) in salah and introduces a critical distinction unique to the Hanafi school: between makruh tahrimi (prohibition-like dislike) and makruh tanzihi (preferable-to-avoid dislike). Understanding this distinction is essential for evaluating when a salah must be repeated and why.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic and legal definitions of makruh
The passage defines makruh linguistically as the opposite of mahbub, and legally as what has a speculative prohibition (zanni) for tahrimi, or what indicates leaving without insistence for tanzihi.
The legal definition is based on the strength of evidence.
2
Distinguish between the two types of karahah based on evidence
If the evidence is a speculative prohibition, it is makruh tahrimi unless a diverting factor exists. If the evidence indicates leaving without insistence, it is makruh tanzihi.
A diverting factor (sarif) can change tahrimi to tanzihi.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi difference on definition
Imam Muhammad defines tahrimi as requiring wajib repetition; the Shaykhayn consider it closer to tanzihi in some cases. The mufta bihi follows Imam Muhammad.
This difference affects the practical ruling.
4
Apply the consequence: when is repetition wajib vs. mustahabb?
If the karahah involves leaving a wajib, repetition is wajib. If it involves leaving a sunnah, repetition is mustahabb.
The salah is valid in both cases but repetition removes the dislike.
5
Examine the hadith interpretation that supports the ruling
The hadith 'Do not pray after a similar prayer' is interpreted as prohibiting repetition due to waswasa, not due to karahah, so repetition for karahah is allowed.
This interpretation prevents conflict with the principle of avoiding waswasa.

Chapter on Disliked Acts. The disliked (makruh) is the opposite of the beloved. What has a speculative prohibition (zanni) is makruh tahrimi unless there is a diverting factor. If the evidence is not a prohibition but indicates leaving without insistence, it is makruh tanzihi. Makruh tanzihi is closer to permissible, and makruh tahrimi is closer to forbidden. The salah is repeated: if it is valid due to leaving a wajib, it is repeated wajiban; if due to leaving something else, it is repeated istihbaban. Al-Tajnis said: Every salah performed with karahah is repeated without that karahah. The Prophet's saying, 'Do not pray after a similar prayer,' is interpreted as a prohibition of repetition due to waswasa, so it does not include repetition due to karahah. Sadr al-Islam al-Bazdawi mentioned this. ... [Further commentary on definitions and distinctions]
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on disliked acts (makruhat) in salah. It defines makruh linguistically as the opposite of mahbub (beloved) and legally as an act prohibited by a speculative (zanni) evidence. Such an act is makruh tahrimi unless there is a factor diverting it from prohibition. If the evidence does not indicate a prohibition but merely suggests leaving the act without insistence, it is makruh tanzihi. Makruh tanzihi is closer to permissible, while makruh tahrimi is closer to forbidden. A salah performed with karahah must be repeated: wajib (obligatory) if a wajib was left, mustahabb (recommended) if a sunnah was left. The author references al-Tajnis: every salah performed with karahah must be repeated without the karahah. The hadith 'Do not pray after a similar prayer' is interpreted as prohibiting repetition due to waswasa, not repetition due to karahah. This is from Sadr al-Islam al-Bazdawi. The passage also discusses the criteria for distinguishing between the two types of karahah based on the strength of the evidence and the nature of the act. It mentions that if an act involves leaving a wajib, it is makruh tahrimi; if it involves leaving a sunnah, it is makruh tanzihi. If it is extraneous and does not complete the prayer or remove harm, it is also makruh. The commentary also gives examples from the earlier section about intimate contact, and includes a discussion about the definition of makruh according to Imam Muhammad and the Shaykhayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad).

Key Terms
المكروه (al-makruh): An act whose avoidance is preferable; legally, it is what is prohibited by speculative evidence (zanni) or what is better to leave without prohibition.
الكراهة التحريمية (al-karahah al-tahrimiyyah): Dislike that approaches prohibition; performing such an act in salah requires wajib repetition if a wajib is left.
الكراهة التنزيهية (al-karahah al-tanzihiyyah): Dislike that is less severe; performing such an act in salah recommends repetition (mustahabb) if a sunnah is left.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when must a salah performed with karahah be repeated wajiban?
A) When it is performed with makruh tanzihi.
B) When a wajib of the salah is omitted.
C) When a sunnah is omitted.
D) Whenever any karahah is present.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school uniquely distinguishes makruh tahrimi (requires wajib repetition) from makruh tanzihi (recommends repetition) based on the strength of evidence and the nature of the omitted act.

Salah — Prayer
intermediate
7 min
Classification of Karahah and Repetition of Prayer
Explains distinction between makruh tahrimi and tanzihi based on evidence and repetition rules.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between makruh tahrimi and makruh tanzihi in prayer based on the nature of the evidence (dalil), and state the ruling on repetition for each type.

Core Idea
Not all disliked acts in prayer require repetition: only makruh tahrimi (omission of a wajib) necessitates it; makruh tanzihi (omission of a sunnah) makes repetition recommended, not obligatory.

In Hanafi fiqh, acts that are disliked (makruh) in prayer are not all treated equally. This lesson explores the critical distinction between makruh tahrimi and tanzihi, based on the strength of the textual evidence. Understanding this difference is essential for correctly determining when a prayer must be repeated and when repetition is merely recommended. The commentary from al-Tahtawi's Hashiya provides the authoritative clarification, addressing a potential counter-argument from a hadith.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of karahah
Name the two types: makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked) and makruh tanzihi (lightly disliked). Note their respective closeness to prohibition and permissibility.
Makruh tahrimi is closer to haram, tanzihi is closer to halal.
2
Examine the dalil criterion
The key factor is the nature of the evidence. If there is a prohibitive text (nahy) with zanni thubut (probable transmission), it is tahrimi. If the evidence merely suggests leaving the act without certainty, it is tanzihi.
The dalil's strength determines the type of karahah.
3
Analyze the ruling on repetition
If a wajib (obligatory element) is omitted, the prayer is valid but must be repeated as an obligation (wajib). If a sunnah is omitted, repetition is recommended (mustahabb) to remove the karahah.
Repetition for wajib is wajib; for sunnah, it is mustahabb.
4
Reconcile the hadith objection
The hadith 'Do not pray after a prayer like it' seems to forbid repetition. The Hanafi response: it refers to repetition due to waswasa (obsessive doubts), not to repetition due to karahah.
This interpretation preserves the distinction between legitimate and illegitimate repetition.
5
Note intra-madhhab variation
The Shaykhayn (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) categorize karahah differently based on strength of dalil, while Imam Muhammad's view forms the basis for the tahrimi/tanzihi distinction. The later fatwa accepts both approaches.
Al-Tahtawi synthesizes these views.

أجنبياً وليس فيه تتميم لها، ولا دفع ضرر فهو مكروه أيضاً كالعبث بالثوب، أو البدن وكل ما يشغل القلب، وكذا ما هو من عادة أهل التكبر، وصنيع أهل الكتاب وكراهة ذلك على حسب ما يقتضيه الدليل، فإن كان الدليل مفيداً للنهي الظني الثبوت فالكراهة تحريمية إلا إذا وجد له صارف عن التحريم، وإن لم يفد النهي بل كان مفيداً للترك من غير جزم فتنزيهية، وأما إذا كان فهي تتميم لها فذكر في الخلاصة أنه لو لم تمكنه عمامته من السجود فرفعها بيد واحدة، أو سواها كذلك لا يكره لأنه من متممات الصلاة، أو كان فيه دفع ضرر كقتل الحية، والعقرب فإنه لا يكره كما في الحلبي قوله : (إلا) لصارف كقوله : إذا قام أحدكم في الصلاة فلا يغمض عينيه فإنه نهى صرف عن ظاهره لأن الكراهة لتفويت النظر المندوب في الصلاة فتكون للتنزيه. قوله : ( وإن لم يكن الدليل نهياً الخ) كقول عمر رضي الله عنه لمن رآه يصلي في ثياب البذلة، أرأيت لو كنت أرسلتك إلى بعض الناس أكنت تمر في ثيابك هذه، فقال) لا، فقال الله أحق أن تتزين له . عمر قوله: (والمكروه) تنزيهاً الخ) هذا على رأي الشيخين كما علمت من كلام صاحب التلويح كما أن أول الكلام على رأي محمد قوله : (مع كونها صحيحة لإستجماع شرائطها كذا في الشرح قوله : (لترك واجب وجوباً) في الوقت وبعده ندباً كذا في الدر أول قضاء الفوائت. قوله: (وتعاد استحباباً بترك غيره أي السنة وظاهر اطلاقه ندبها، ولو بعد الوقت دفعاً للكراهة . قوله : أديت مع الكراهة (الخ وجه الاستدلال أنه أطلق الكراهة فعم التحريمية، والتنزيهية. قوله : (تأويله النهي عن الإعادة الخ) أو النهي عن المماثلة في القراءة، أو عن تكرارها في الجماعة، وهذا من تتمة كلام صاحب التجنيس لا من كلام المؤلف .
If the evidence is not a prohibition, but rather indicates abandonment without certainty, then it is [makruh] tanzihi, and makruh tanzihi is closer to permissibility, while makruh tahrimi is closer to prohibition. The prayer is repeated, while being valid, for omitting a wajib as an obligation, and repeated as recommended for omitting other than that. He said in al-Tajnis: Every prayer performed with karahah is repeated, not in a karahah manner. And his saying (peace be upon him): 'Do not pray after a prayer like it' — its interpretation is the prohibition of repetition due to waswasa, so it does not include repetition due to karahah. This was mentioned by Sadr al-Islam al-Bazdawi in... (continued).
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This passage from al-Tahtawi's hashya clarifies the Hanafi classification of karahah (disliked acts) in prayer. It distinguishes between makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked) and makruh tanzihi (lightly disliked) based on the nature of the evidence (dalil). If the evidence implies a clear prohibition (nahy) with zanni thubut (probable transmission), the act is makruh tahrimi; if it merely suggests leaving the act without certainty, it is makruh tanzihi. The text then addresses the ruling on repeating prayers performed with karahah: if a wajib (obligatory element) is omitted, the prayer must be repeated (wajib), but it remains valid; if a sunnah is omitted, repetition is recommended (mustahabb) to remove the dislike. It cites al-Tajnis to support this. A potential objection is raised using the hadith 'Do not pray after a prayer like it,' which might seem to forbid repetition altogether. The commentator explains that this hadith refers to repetition due to waswasa (whisperings), not repetition due to karahah. The passage also discusses the views of the Shaykhayn (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) and Imam Muhammad, noting that the distinction between tahrimi and tanzihi follows the latter's methodology. The authority of this analysis is from the later Hanafi tradition, specifically al-Tahtawi's commentary.

Key Terms
كراهة تحريمية (karāha taḥrīmiyya): Dislike that approaches prohibition; requires repetition of prayer if the disliked act is done intentionally. Based on a prohibitive evidence (nahy) with zanni thubut.
كراهة تنزيهية (karāha tanzīhiyya): Light dislike, closer to permissibility; does not render a prayer invalid or require repetition, though repetition is recommended to remove the dislike.
الوسوسة (al-waswasa): Whisperings from Satan or obsessive doubts that lead to unnecessary repetition in prayer. The hadith prohibits repetition due to waswasa.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary factor that determines whether an act is makruh tahrimi or makruh tanzihi?
A) The act being connected to a pillar (rukn)
B) The nature of the evidence (dalil) and whether it implies a clear prohibition
C) The number of times the act is performed
D) The intention of the person praying

Key Takeaway
Distinguishing between makruh tahrimi and makruh tanzihi in prayer hinges on the strength of the evidence: a prohibitive proof (zanni) yields tahrimi requiring repetition; a mere discouragement yields tanzihi making repetition recommended.

The Ruling on Disliked Acts in Prayer: Fidgeting and Related Actions
Lists specific makruh acts in prayer with evidence and commentary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state which acts are makruh in prayer according to al-Jami' al-Saghir and identify the evidence that establishes the karahah.

Core Idea
Fidgeting in prayer is makruh tahrimi because it violates khushu', the spirit of prayer.

The passage from al-Jami' al-Saghir, as expanded in the hashya, lists approximately seventy-seven disliked acts in prayer, emphasizing fidgeting (al-'abath). The commentary explains why such actions are prohibited, citing the Quranic command for khushu' and specific hadith warnings. Understanding these rulings helps preserve the essence of prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general principle
The passage begins by stating that omitting a wajib or sunnah intentionally is disliked, and this applies to many specifics like tranquility in pillars.
This sets a universal rule for the examples that follow.
2
Recognize key examples
The text lists racing the imam, improper hand placement, covering feet in sujud for men, fidgeting with clothes or body, turning pebbles, and cracking fingers.
These are specific instances of the general principle.
3
Locate the evidence
The author cites Quran 23:1-2 on khushu', the hadith 'Allah dislikes fidgeting in prayer', and the incident of the Prophet seeing a man fidgeting with his beard.
The dalil consists of qat'i thubut but zanni dalala, making the ruling makruh tahrimi.
4
Understand the ruling classification
The commentary explicitly states 'فكان مكروهاً أي تحريماً' for fidgeting, indicating it is prohibitively disliked. Racing the imam also carries a strong threat.
Some acts may be makruh tanzihi, but the passage highlights the stronger dislike.
5
Note exceptions
Fidgeting due to legitimate need (e.g., wiping sweat) is not disliked; turning pebbles once for sujud is allowed.
These exceptions prevent undue hardship.

Commentary
This passage from the hashya presents the matn of al-Jami' al-Saghir listing disliked acts in prayer. It states that approximately seventy-seven things are makruh for the praying person, but the number is not fixed. The commentator then expands on several examples: deliberately omitting a wajib or sunnah (which includes missing tranquility in pillars or racing the imam), raising hands beyond the ears, placing hands below the shoulders, and covering the feet in sujud for men. The main focus is on fidgeting ('abath) which contradicts khushu' (humility and presence of heart). The commentary provides Quranic and hadith evidence for the prohibition of fidgeting: verses on khushu', the hadith 'Allah dislikes fidgeting in prayer', and the incident of the man fidgeting with his beard. It distinguishes between 'abath (purposeless action) and actions that serve a legitimate need, such as wiping sweat or removing harm. Also covered are turning pebbles (allowed only once for sujud, otherwise makruh) and cracking fingers. The commentary cites various authorities like al-Nihayah, al-'Inayah, Fath al-Qadir, and al-Kardari. The overall ruling is that such acts are makruh, with some being makruh tahrimi (like racing the imam) due to the strong warning in the sahih hadith.

Key Terms
العبث (al-'abath): Any action in prayer that serves no legitimate purpose, such as fidgeting with the body or clothes, which contradicts khushu'.
الخشوع (al-khushu'): Humility and presence of heart in prayer, coupled with stillness of the limbs. It is considered the spirit of prayer.
قلب الحصا (qalb al-hasa): Turning or smoothing pebbles on the place of prostration. It is makruh unless done once to enable proper sujud.

Key Takeaway
Fidgeting in prayer is makruh tahrimi because it violates the commanded khushu', but it does not invalidate the prayer.

Ruling on Fidgeting (Al-‘Abath) in Prayer
Ruling and proof that fidgeting in prayer is makruh.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on fidgeting in prayer, identify its proofs, and apply the exception for genuine need.

Core Idea
Fidgeting in prayer is makruh tahrimi because it contradicts khushu', unless there is a genuine need.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on unnecessary movement in prayer. The text from Hashiya al-Tahtawi clarifies that fidgeting contradicts the essence of prayer—khushu'—and is thus disliked. However, the scholars make a critical distinction: fidgeting due to genuine need (like wiping sweat) is not disliked. Understanding this nuance helps avoid both excess and rigidity in worship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is al-‘abath? The text says it is any action with no benefit and not from the actions of prayer. The ruling is makruh tahrimi.
Hanafi terminology: makruh tahrimi is close to haram but based on a zanni proof.
2
Locate the proofs
Two Quranic verses: the command for khushu' (23:1-2) and a hadith qudsi stating Allah dislikes fidgeting in prayer. Also a hadith where the Prophet (saw) rebukes a man fidgeting with his beard.
The proofs are from Quran and Sunnah.
3
Understand the wajh al-dalalah
The verse commands khushu', which is the spirit of prayer; fidgeting contradicts khushu'. The hadith explicitly states dislike, indicating prohibition (karahah).
The command for khushu' implies that violating it is makruh.
4
Note the definition and exceptions
The text defines abath precisely and then cites that fidgeting is only disliked if there is no need. If need arises (like sweating or dirt), it is not disliked.
The exception is based on the principle of need overriding the default ruling.
5
Evaluate the hierarchy
The ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah, and the mufta bihi is that it is makruh tahrimi unless need. Some schools consider excessive movement invalidating, but Hanafis hold it as makruh.
Authority weight: zahir riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كعبثه بثوبه وبدنه لأنه ينافي الخشوع الذي هو روح الصلاة فكان مكروهاً لقوله تعالى : وقد أفلح المؤمنون الذين هم في صلاتهم خاشعون وقوله : «إن الله تعالى كره لكم العبث في الصلاة والرفث في الصيام والضحك عند المقابر ورأى عليه الصلاة والسلام رجلاً يعبث بلحيته في الصلاة فقال : لو خشع قلبه لخشعت جوارحه، والعبث عمل لا فائدة فيه، ولا حكمة تقتضيه والمراد بالعبث هنا فعل ما ليس من أفعال الصلاة لأنه ينافيها قوله : (والعبث الخ) قال بدر الدين الكردري : العبث ما لا غرض فيه شرعاً، والسفه ما لا غرض فيه أصلاً، وفي الجوهرة العبث ما لا لذة فيه، وما فيه لذة فهو اللعب اهـ، وعبارة الصحاح تفيد الترادف بين العبث واللعب قوله: (فعل ما ليس من أفعال الصلاة) قال في النهاية والعناية، وفتح القدير : إنما يكره العبث في الصلاة إذا لم تدع الحاجة إليه فإن دعت فلا بأس به كسلت العرق عن وجهه أو التراب عند الإيذاء
Like his fidgeting with his garment and his body because it contradicts khushu' which is the spirit of prayer, so it is makruh due to His statement: 'Successful indeed are the believers who are in their prayer with khushu'' and His statement: 'Indeed Allah has disliked for you fidgeting in prayer, obscenity in fasting, and laughing at graves' and he saw a man fidgeting with his beard in prayer and said: 'If his heart had humbled, his limbs would have humbled.' And al-‘abath is an action with no benefit and no wisdom that necessitates it, and the meaning of al-‘abath here is doing what is not from the actions of prayer because it contradicts it. His statement: (and al-‘abath etc.) Badr al-Din al-Kardari said: al-‘abath is what has no legitimate purpose, and al-safah is what has no purpose at all. In al-Jawhara: al-‘abath has no pleasure, and what has pleasure is la‘b. The expression of al-Sihah indicates synonymy between al-‘abath and al-la‘b. His statement: (doing what is not from the actions of prayer) He said in al-Nihaya, al-‘Inaya, and Fath al-Qadir: Fidgeting in prayer is only disliked if there is no need for it; if there is need, then it is fine, like wiping sweat from the face or dirt when it is bothersome.
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Hashiya of al-Tahtawi on Maraqi al-Falah. It addresses the category of al-‘abath (fidgeting) in prayer. The matn (original text of Maraqi al-Falah) states that fidgeting with one's clothing or body is disliked (makruh) because it contradicts khushu', which is the essence of prayer. The proof is provided from two Quranic verses: one commanding khushu' in prayer (Quran 23:1-2), and an explicit statement that Allah dislikes fidgeting in prayer (from a hadith qudsi). Additionally, a hadith of the Prophet (peace be upon him) is cited: seeing a man fidgeting with his beard, he said that if his heart were humble, his limbs would be still. The commentator defines al-‘abath as an action with no benefit or wisdom, and distinguishes it from 'play' (la‘b) based on pleasure. He then clarifies that fidgeting is only disliked when there is no genuine need; if there is a need (like wiping off sweat or dirt), it is not disliked. This applies only during the prayer; after prayer, it is not disliked.

Key Terms
العبث (al-‘abath): An action in prayer that has no benefit or wisdom, and is not from the actions of prayer.
الخشوع (al-khushu'): Presence of heart, stillness of limbs, and preservation of the pillars.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) It is makruh tahrimi unless there is a genuine need.
C) It is permissible (mubah) as long as it is not excessive.
D) It is sunnah to fidget to maintain alertness.

Key Takeaway
Fidgeting in prayer is makruh tahrimi because it destroys khushu', but if there is a genuine need, it is not disliked.

Turning Pebbles in Prayer: Ruling and Proof
Ruling and proof that turning pebbles in prayer is makruh except for sujud.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on turning pebbles in prayer and explain the evidence from the hadith, including the exception for sujud.

Core Idea
Turning pebbles in prayer is disliked except when needed for sujud — one movement is permitted but leaving it is better.

In prayer, we are encouraged to minimize extra movements to maintain focus (khushu'). One common action is adjusting the ground, such as turning pebbles. The Hanafi school has a specific ruling on this, based on a hadith from the Prophet ﷺ. This lesson unpacks that ruling and its proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is 'qalb al-hasa' (turning pebbles). The ruling is makruh tanzihi (disliked but not prohibited), except when done for sujud (prostration).
The exception covers cases where adjusting the ground is necessary for a proper prostration.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites a hadith: Jabir asked the Prophet about wiping pebbles, and the Prophet said 'one (movement), and that refraining is better for you than a hundred black-eyed camels.'
This hadith is ahad (single-chain) but considered reliable.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The Prophet permitted one movement, showing permissibility. But the phrase 'refraining is better' indicates that leaving it is superior, which in Hanafi usul makes the action makruh tanzihi (disliked).
The contrast with a valuable gift emphasizes avoiding it is rewarded.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some schools (e.g., Shafi'i) consider any unnecessary movement as potentially invalidating if excessive. The Hanafi school distinguishes: one movement is permissible, more than one is disliked.
The exception for sujud is agreed upon by most schools.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi position relies on the apparent meaning of the hadith: permission and recommendation to avoid. This is the mufta bihi (operative) view.
No major intra-Hanafi dispute on this ruling.

وقلب الحصا إلا للسجود (مرة قال جابر بن عبدالله سألت النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم عن مسح الحصا فقال واحدة، ولأن تمسك عنها خير لك مائة ناقة سود الحدق قوله : (وقلب الحصا) بالقصر جمع حصاة الحجارة الصغار. قوله : (إلا للسجود) أي ليتمكن من السجود التام أما إذا لم يمكنه أصل السجود فيجب كما في النهر. قوله : (قال جابر الخ) وقال سألته أبو ذر : سألت النبي عن كل شيء حتى الحصا فقال : واحدة أودع وقال الكردري: في ذلك سجعاً، وهو سأل أبو ذر خير البشر عن تسوية الحجر فقال : يا أبا ذر مرة وإلا قذر كما في السراج، وغاية البيان فما يروي يا أبا ذر مرة وإلا فذر من الرواية بالمعنى
And turning pebbles, except for sujud (once). Jabir ibn Abdillah said: I asked the Prophet about wiping pebbles, and he said: 'One (movement), and that refraining from it is better for you than one hundred black-eyed camels.' His statement: (and turning pebbles) with qasr (shortened form), plural of hasat (small stones). His statement: (except for sujud) meaning to enable complete sujud; but if he cannot perform sujud at all, then it becomes obligatory as in al-Nahr. His statement: (Jabir etc.) And he said: Abu Dharr asked him: I asked the Prophet about everything, even pebbles, and he said: 'One (movement) or leave.' And al-Kardari said: There is alliteration in that: Abu Dharr asked the best of mankind about leveling the stone, and he said: 'O Abu Dharr, once, or else filth.' As in al-Siraj and Ghayat al-Bayan, so what is narrated 'once or else leave' is narration by meaning.
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on the action of turning or smoothing pebbles (or dirt) in prayer. The matn states that it is disliked (makruh) except for the purpose of sujud (prostration). The proof comes from a hadith where Jabir asked the Prophet about wiping pebbles, and the Prophet permitted one movement but emphasized that leaving it is better. The commentator clarifies that 'qalb al-hasa' refers to small stones. The exception 'for sujud' means to enable a proper prostration; if the ground is uneven and prevents sujud, then it becomes obligatory to adjust it. The hadith variant from Abu Dharr is also mentioned, with the meaning that one may do it once or leave it, indicating the dislike of excessive movement.

Key Terms
قلب الحصا (qalb al-hasa): Turning or smoothing small stones or dirt on the ground in prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on turning pebbles (qalb al-hasa) during prayer?
A) It is haram and invalidates the prayer.
B) It is permissible without any restrictions.
C) It is makruh tanzihi, except for enabling sujud where it is not disliked.
D) It is sunnah to do it to keep the place clean.

Key Takeaway
Turning pebbles in prayer is makruh tanzihi unless necessary for sujud, based on the hadith permitting one movement but advising against it.

Salah — Prayer
intermediate
8 min
Ruling on Cracking and Interlocking Fingers in Prayer
Ruling and proof that cracking and interlocking fingers in prayer is makruh tahrimi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on cracking and interlocking fingers in prayer, identify the dalil, and distinguish between the ruling inside and outside prayer.

Core Idea
Cracking and interlocking fingers in prayer is makruh tahrimi due to explicit prophetic prohibition and resemblance to the punished.

Many people unconsciously crack or interlock their fingers during salah, unaware that the Hanafi school considers it a prohibited action. This lesson examines the textual evidence and the reasoning behind this ruling. You will learn why these actions are discouraged even when waiting for prayer or walking to the mosque, and how the ruling changes outside of prayer.

Guided Path
1
State the act and its ruling in prayer
Identify the two acts: cracking fingers (farqa‘at al-asabi‘) and interlocking fingers (tashbik al-asabi‘). Their ruling during prayer in the Hanafi school is makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked).
Makruh tahrimi is close to haram and requires avoidance.
2
Identify the primary dalil
The main evidence is the hadith: 'Do not crack your fingers while you are in prayer' (لا تفرقع أصابعك وأنت في الصلاة). For interlocking, the hadith of Ibn Umar states that it is the prayer of those with whom Allah is angry.
Both hadith are from the Sunnah (ahad), with zanni thubut and dalala.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
The imperative negative 'la taf'al' in the hadith indicates prohibition (tahrim) in Hanafi usul. The specific context of prayer makes the prohibition operative during the prayer state.
Outside prayer, the same action is only makruh tanzihi because the prohibition is tied to prayer.
4
Distinguish the ruling outside prayer
Cracking fingers outside prayer is only makruh tanzihi (lightly disliked) because it is a type of fidgeting but not explicitly prohibited. Interlocking fingers outside prayer is also permissible, though disliked if it resembles shaytan.
The distinction is based on the strength of the evidence in different contexts.
5
Apply to related situations
The ruling extends to one who is waiting for prayer or walking to the mosque, as they are considered in a state of prayer according to hadith. Thus, cracking or interlocking fingers in those situations is also makruh tahrimi.
After prayer, there is no prohibition, and interlocking is permissible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفرقعة الأصابع) ولو من مرة وهو غمزها أو مذها حتى تصوت لقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم : لا تفرقع أصابعك وأنت في الصلاة (وتشبيكها) لقول ابن عمر فيه تلك صلاة المغضوب عليهم والتخصر) لأنه نهى عنه في قوله : (لا تفرقع الخ) هذا يفيد التحريم، وألحق في المجتبى منتظر الصلاة، والماشي إليها بمن فيها، وأما خارج الصلاة ففي القهستاني، وتكره خارج الصلاة عند كثيرين، وعلله في المجتبى كما في البحر بأنها من الشيطان لكن قال : لما لم يكن فيها خارجها فهي لم تكن تحريمية، وعلل في البرهان الكراهة بأنه نوع من العبث وقال : الضاحك في الصلاة والملتفت والمفرقع أصابعه سواء يعني في الإثم كذا في مجمع الروايات، وإنما كره لأنه عمل قوم لوط فيكره التشبه بهم، قال صلى الله عليه وسلم لعلي : «أني أحب لك ما أحب لنفسي لا تفرقع أصابعك وأنت تصلي، كذا في المستصفى. قوله : (وتشبيكها ولو حال السعي إلى الصلاة لما روى أحمد، وأبو داود، وغيرهما مرفوعاً، إذا توضأ أحدكم فأحسن وضوءه، ثم خرج عامداً إلى المسجد، فلا يشبك بين يديه فإنه في صلاة، وإذا كان منتظراً لها بالأولى، والذي يظهر أنها أيضاً تحريمية للنهي المذكور كما في البحر، وأما إذا انصرف من الصلاة فلا بأس به وحكمة النهي عن التشبيك أنه من الشيطان كما في الحديث، وأنه يجلب النوم، وهو من مظان الحدث، وأن صورة التشبيك تشبه صورة الاختلاف كما نبه عليه في حديث ابن عمر فكره ذلك لما هو في حكم الصلاة حتى لا يقع في
And cracking the fingers, even once, which is pressing or pulling them until they make a sound, due to his saying: 'Do not crack your fingers while you are in prayer' (and interlocking them) due to the saying of Ibn Umar: 'That is the prayer of those with whom Allah is angry.' And (placing hands on hips) because he forbade it. His statement: (do not crack) This indicates prohibition. In al-Mujtaba, the one waiting for prayer and the one walking to it are joined to one in prayer. Outside of prayer, it is said in al-Qahistani: it is disliked outside of prayer according to many. And he explains in al-Mujtaba, as in al-Bahr, that it is from the shaytan, but he said: since it is not in prayer, it is not prohibited. And in al-Burhan, the dislike is explained as a type of fidgeting, and he said: 'The one who laughs in prayer, the one who turns away, and the one who cracks his fingers are the same in sin.' In Majma' al-Riwayat, it is disliked because it is the action of the people of Lut, so imitating them is disliked. The Prophet said to Ali: 'I love for you what I love for myself: do not crack your fingers while you pray.' This is in al-Mustasfa. His statement: (and interlocking them, even while walking to prayer) because Ahmad, Abu Dawud, and others narrated marfu'an: 'When one of you performs wudu and does it well, then goes out intending the mosque, let him not interlock his fingers, for he is in prayer.' And when waiting, it is even more so. And it appears that this is also prohibited due to the mentioned prohibition as in al-Bahr. After prayer, it is fine. The wisdom behind the prohibition of interlocking is that it is from the shaytan, and it brings sleep, which is a cause for invalidating ablution, and the form of interlocking resembles the form of disagreement as indicated in the hadith of Ibn Umar, so it is disliked because he is in the state of prayer, so that he does not fall into...
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling on both cracking fingers (farqa‘at al-asabi‘) and interlocking fingers (tashbik al-asabi‘) in prayer. The matn states that both are disliked. The proof for cracking fingers is the explicit hadith: 'Do not crack your fingers while you are in prayer.' The commentator notes that the wording indicates prohibition (tahrim). This ruling also applies to one who is waiting for prayer or walking to the mosque, as they are considered in a state of prayer. Outside of prayer, cracking fingers is only disliked, not prohibited. The commentator mentions that it is considered a type of fidgeting and that the one who does it shares the sin with those who laugh or turn away in prayer. For interlocking fingers, the hadith from Ibn Umar calls it 'the prayer of those with whom Allah is angry,' and other hadith prohibit it while walking to the mosque. The wisdom includes that it is from shaytan, brings sleep (which risks breaking wudu), and resembles disagreement. After prayer, it is permissible.

Key Terms
فرقعة الأصابع (farqa‘at al-asabi‘): Cracking the fingers by pressing or pulling them to produce a sound.
تشبيك الأصابع (tashbik al-asabi‘): Interlocking the fingers of both hands together.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on cracking fingers (farqa‘at al-asabi‘) during prayer?
A) It is permissible.
B) It is makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked).
C) It is mustahab (recommended).
D) It invalidates the prayer.

Key Takeaway
Cracking or interlocking fingers during prayer is a prohibited act (makruh tahrimi) based on explicit hadith, and the ruling extends to waiting for prayer and walking to the mosque.
